The Five Preventers (Mawani') in Qiyas: A Hanafi Classification
Classifies five types of preventers that block the ruling

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify all five types of preventers in qiyas, distinguish them from specification of the cause, and apply each to a relevant example.

Core Idea
A preventer blocks the ruling at different stages—formation, completion, beginning, completion, or bindingness.

In this lesson, we examine the Hanafi classification of five preventers (mawani') that block the application of a ruling in qiyas. The author builds on the earlier discussion of specification of the cause, clarifying that some preventers do not actually involve a fully existing cause. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correctly applying qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Understand the background
The passage builds on the discussion of specification of the cause (takhsis al-illah). The author corrects a previous classification by Fakhr al-Islam.
Specification assumes the cause exists but the ruling is absent; here, the cause itself may be absent or incomplete.
2
Identify the five types
The five types are: (1) prevents formation of the cause, (2) prevents completion of the cause, (3) prevents beginning of the ruling, (4) prevents completion of the ruling, (5) prevents bindingness of the ruling.
Each type has a distinct example from sale contracts.
3
Differentiate first two from specification
The author criticizes counting the first two as specification because the cause is not legally existent. They are better seen as absence or incompletion of the cause.
Sadr al-Shariah (author of al-Tawdih) refined the classification into five categories of 'what necessitates the absence of the ruling.'
4
Apply each type to its example
Associate each preventer with its example: sale of a free man (formation), sale of another's slave without permission (completion), option of stipulation (beginning), option of inspection (completion), option of defect (bindingness).
5
See the transition
The passage ends by introducing the next topic: types of causes in qiyas (tardiyyah and mu'aththirah) and modes of rebuttal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبيان ذلك: في الصائم إذا صب الماء في فيه... ويلزم عليه الناسي. وقلنا: امتنع الحكم الخ وبنى على هذا -أي على بحث تخصيص العلة بالمانع- تقسيم الموائع وهي خمسة: مانع يمنع انعقاد العلة كبيع الحر، فإنه إذا باع الحر لا ينعقد البيع شرعًا وإن وجد صورة. ومانع يمنع تمام العلة كبيع عبد الغير بلا إذنه، فإنه ينعقد شرعًا لوجود المحل ولكنه لا يتم ما لم يوجد رضاء المالك. وعد هذين القسمين من قبيل تخصيص العلة مسامحة نشأت من فخر الإسلام لأن التخصيص هو تكلف الحكم مع وجود العلة، ولم توجد العلة إلا أن يقال إنها التخصيص وجدت صورة وإن لم يعتبر شرعًا. ولهذا عدل صاحب التوضيح إلى أن جملة ما يوجب عدم الحكم خمسة لئلا يرد عليه هذا الاعتراض. ومانع يمنع ابتداء الحكم كخيار الشرط في البيع، فإنه وجدت العلة بتمامها ولكن لم يبدأ الحكم وهو الملك للخيار. ومانع يمنع تمام الحكم كخيار الرؤية، فإنه لا يمنع ثبوت الملك ولكن لم يتم [؟] معه، ولهذا يتمكن من له الخيار من فسخ العقد دون قضاء أو رضاء. ومانع يمنع لزوم الحكم كخيار العيب، فإنه لا يمنع ثبوت الملك ولا تمامه حتى يتمكن المشتري من التصرف في المبيع، ولا يتمكن من الفسخ دون قضاء. ثم لما فرغ المصنف عن بيان شرط القياس وركنه وحكمه شرع في بيان دفعه فقال: ثم العلل نوعان: طردية ومؤثرة، وعلى كل قسم ضروب من الدفع.
And its explanation is: concerning the fasting person if water is poured into his mouth... and he is required to [be like] the forgetful. And we said: the ruling is prevented. And based on this – i.e., on the discussion of specification of the cause by the preventer – is the division of preventers, and they are five: A preventer that prevents the formation of the cause, like the sale of a free man; for if a free man sells, the sale is not legally concluded, even though its form exists. And a preventer that prevents the completion of the cause, like the sale of another's slave without his permission; for it is legally concluded due to the existence of the subject matter, but it is not completed until the owner's consent is obtained. And counting these two types under the specification of the cause is a laxity that emanated from Fakhr al-Islam, because specification is the ruling being absent despite the existence of the cause; but here the cause is not existent, unless it is said that [the cause] exists in form even though it is not considered legally. For this reason, the author of al-Tawdih changed [the classification] to say that the total of what necessitates the absence of the ruling is five, so that this objection does not arise against him. And a preventer that prevents the beginning of the ruling, like the option of stipulation in a sale; for the cause exists in its entirety but the ruling – which is ownership – does not commence due to the option. And a preventer that prevents the completion of the ruling, like the option of inspection; for it does not prevent the establishment of ownership, but it is not completed with it; hence the one with the option can rescind the contract without a court order or consent. And a preventer that prevents the bindingness of the ruling, like the option of defect; for it does not prevent the establishment of ownership nor its completion such that the buyer can dispose of the sold item, but he cannot rescind without a court order. Then when the author finished explaining the condition, pillar, and ruling of qiyas, he began explaining its rebuttal, saying: Then the causes are of two types: formal (tardiyyah) and effective (mu'aththirah), and for each type there are kinds of rebuttal.
Source pages p. 179

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi usul classification of preventers (mawani') that block the application of a ruling derived by qiyas. The author builds on the earlier discussion of specification of the cause by a preventer (takhsis al-illah bi al-mani'). First, he notes that the case of the forgetful person in fasting is resolved not by specification but by saying the cause itself is absent. Then he introduces the five types of preventers: (1) prevents the formation of the cause—e.g., a free man cannot be sold, so the cause (valid sale) never forms; (2) prevents the completion of the cause—e.g., selling another's slave without permission, the cause exists but is incomplete until consent; (3) prevents the beginning of the ruling—e.g., stipulation of an option in a sale, the cause exists but ownership does not begin; (4) prevents the completion of the ruling—e.g., the option of inspection, ownership exists but not fully settled; (5) prevents the bindingness of the ruling—e.g., the option of defect, ownership is complete but the contract is not binding until the option lapses. The author criticizes Fakhr al-Islam al-Bazdawi for counting the first two as specification of the cause, noting that they are better seen as absence or incompletion of the cause itself. The author of al-Tawdih (Sadr al-Shariah) therefore refined the classification into five general categories of 'what necessitates the absence of the ruling.' Finally, the passage transitions to the next topic: types of causes in qiyas (tardiyyah and mu'aththirah) and modes of rebuttal.

Key Terms
المانع (al-māni'): A legal preventer that blocks the ruling of a cause at various stages (formation, completion, beginning, completion of effect, or bindingness).
تخصيص العلة بالمانع (takhsīṣ al-ʿillah bi al-māni'): Specifying the cause by a preventer—i.e., the ruling is absent despite the cause being present, due to a preventer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which type of preventer (mani') blocks the very beginning of the ruling even though the cause is fully present?
A) Preventer that prevents the formation of the cause
B) Preventer that prevents the completion of the cause
C) Preventer that prevents the beginning of the ruling
D) Preventer that prevents the bindingness of the ruling

Key Takeaway
Preventers in qiyas are of five types, each blocking a different stage: formation, completion, beginning, completion, or bindingness of the ruling.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Two Types of Legal Causes and Qawl bi-Mujib al-Illa
Introduces two types of 'illah and the first method of refutation (qawl bi-mujib al-illa).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two types of legal causes (tardiyyah and mu'aththirah) and apply the method of qawl bi-mujib al-illa to refute a tardiyyah cause.

Core Idea
Qawl bi-mujib al-illa refutes a tardiyyah cause by conceding its consequence while showing it does not yield the opponent's ruling.

In Hanafi usul, not every attribute that accompanies a ruling qualifies as a valid legal cause. This lesson explores the critical distinction between tardiyyah (concomitant) and mu'aththirah (effective) causes, and introduces the first method of refuting the former: qawl bi-mujib al-illa. Through the debate on intention in Ramadan fast, you will see how the Hanafis strategically concede the opponent's premise to expose the weakness of their cause.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two types of 'illah
Understand that a tardiyyah cause is merely concomitant (tard) with the ruling, while a mu'aththirah cause is effective (mu'aththir) and contains a suitable attribute (munasib). Hanafis require mu'aththirah for valid qiyas.
The Shafi'is often use tardiyyah causes; Hanafis refute them.
2
Identify the Shafi'i tardiyyah cause in the example
The Shafi'is argue that because the Ramadan fast is obligatory (fard), it requires explicit specification of intention, analogizing to other obligatory fasts (qada', kaffarah). Their cause is 'fardiyyah' (obligatory nature).
This is a tardiyyah cause because fardiyyah is merely concomitant with the need for specification, not the effective cause.
3
Apply qawl bi-mujib al-illa
The Hanafis accept the consequence that specification is required (mujib al-illa), but they distinguish between two types of specification: intentional (qasdi) by the servant and legal (shar'i) by the Lawgiver. They argue that the month of Ramadan itself, by legal designation, fulfills the specification.
This refutation does not deny the opponent's cause but shows it does not lead to the desired ruling.
4
Analyze the supporting dalil
The Hanafis cite the hadith 'When half of Sha'ban passes, there is no fasting except for Ramadan' as evidence that the Lawgiver has already specified the intention. Thus, a general intention (e.g., 'I intend to fast tomorrow') is sufficient because it is legally specified.
The hadith is ahad (zanni al-thubut) but accepted in usul.
5
Recognize the strategic value of qawl bi-mujib
This method forces the opponent to either accept that their cause is ineffective or concede the Hanafi position. It is a key tool in munazarah (debate) derived from usul al-fiqh.
It is not an admission of defeat but a sophisticated refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ لَمَّا فَرَغَ الْمُصنف عَنْ بَيانِ شَرْطِ الْقَيَاسِ وَرُكْنِهِ وَحُكْمِهِ شَرَعَ فِي بَيانِ دَفْعِهِ فَقَالَ: ثُمَّ الْعِلَلَ نُوْعَانِ: طَرْدِيَّةٌ وَمُؤثِرَةٌ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ قِسْمٍ ضَرُوبٌ مِنَ الدَّفْعِ.
فَإِنَّ الطَّرْدِيَّةَ لِلشَّافِعِيَّةِ وَنَحْنُ نَدْفَعُهَا عَلَى وَجْهٍ يُلْحِقُهُمْ إِلَى الْقَوْلِ بِالتَّأثِيرِ. وَالْمُؤثِرَةُ لَنَا وَتَدْفَعُهَا الشَّافِعِيَّةُ ثُمَّ نُجِيبُهُمْ عَنِ الدَّفْعِ. وَهَذَا الْبَحْثُ هُوَ أَسَاسُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ وَالْمُحَاوَرَةِ وَقَدِ اقْتَبَسَ عِلْمُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ مِنْ هَذَا الْبَحْثِ لِلْأُصُولِ وَجُعِلَ عِلْمًا آخَرَ وَتُصُرِّفَ فِيهِ بِتَغْيِيرِ بَعْضِ الْقَوَاعِدِ وَازْدِيَادِ مَا عَلَى مَا نُبَيِّنُ إِنْشَاءَ الله تَعَالَى.
أَمَّا الطَّرْدِيَّةُ فَوُجُوهٌ دَفْعُهَا أَرْبَعَةٌ: الْأَوَّلُ: الْقَوْلُ بِمُوجِبِ الْعِلَّةِ، أَىْ قَوْلُ الْمُعْتَرِضِ بِمُوجَبِ عِلَّةِ الْمُسْتَدِلِّ، وَهُوَ الْتِزَامُ مَا يَلْزَمُهُ الْمُعَلِّلُ بِتَعْلِيْلِهِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الْخِلافِ فِي الْحُكْمِ الْمُتَنَازَعِ فِيهِ، كَقَوْلِهِمْ (أَىْ قَوْلِ الشَّافِعِيَّةِ) فِي صَوْمِ رَمَضَانَ: إِنَّهُ صَوْمُ فَرْضٍ فَلَا يُؤَدَّى إِلَّا بِتَعْيِينِ النِّيَّةِ بِأَنْ يَقُولَ بِصَوْمٍ غَدًا نَوَيْتُ لِفَرْضِ رَمَضَانَ. فَأَوْرَدُوا الْعِلَّةَ الطَّرْدِيَّةَ وَهِيَ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ لِلتَّعْيِينِ إِذْ إِنَّمَا تُوجَدُ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ يُوجَدُ التَّعْيِينُ كَصَوْمِ الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ وَالصَّلَوَاتِ الْخَمْسِ. وَنَحْنُ نَدْفَعُهُ بِمُوجَبِ عِلَّتِهِ فَنَقُولُ: عِنْدَنَا لَا يَصِحُّ إِلَّا بِتَعْيِينِ النِّيَّةِ، وَإِنَّمَا نُجَوِّزُهُ بِإِطْلَاقِ النِّيَّةِ عَلَى أَنَّهُ تَعْيِينٌ. أَىْ سَلَّمْنَا أَنَّ التَّعْيِينَ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِلْفَرْضِ وَلَكِنَّ التَّعْيِينَ نَوْعَانِ: تَعْيِينٌ مِنْ جَانِبِ الْعِبَادِ قَصْدًا، وَتَعْيِينٌ مِنْ جَانِبِ الشَّارِعِ. هَذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ فِي حُكْمِ التَّعْيِينِ مِنْ جَانِبِ الشَّارِعِ فَإِنَّهُ قَالَ: إِذَا انْتَصَفَ شَعْبَانُ فَلَا صَوْمَ إِلَّا عَنْ رَمَضَانَ. فَإِنْ قَالَ الْخَصْمُ: إِنَّ التَّعْيِينَ الْقَصْدِيَّ هُوَ الْمُعْتَبَرُ عِنْدَنَا كَمَا فِي الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ دُونَ التَّعْيِينِ مُطْلَقًا.
Then, when the author finished explaining the condition, pillar, and ruling of analogy, he began explaining its refutation and said: 'Then legal causes are of two types: tardiyyah (concomitant) and mu'aththirah (effective). For each type there are several methods of refutation. For the Shafi'is use the tardiyyah cause, and we refute it in a way that forces them to accept effectiveness. The mu'aththirah is ours, and the Shafi'is refute it, then we respond to their refutation. This discussion is the foundation of debate and dialogue, and the science of debate was derived from this discussion in usul and made into a separate science, with changes in some rules and additions as we will explain, God willing. As for the tardiyyah, there are four methods of refuting it. The first: qawl bi-mujib al-illa (accepting the consequence of the cause), i.e., the objector accepts the necessary consequence of the proponent's cause, while maintaining the disagreement on the disputed ruling. For example, their saying (i.e., the Shafi'is) concerning the fast of Ramadan: it is an obligatory fast, so it is not performed except with specified intention, by saying 'I intend to fast tomorrow for the obligatory Ramadan.' So they presented the tardiyyah cause, which is obligatory nature (fardiyyah) for specification, since whenever obligatory nature exists, specification exists, like in compensatory fasts, expiatory fasts, and the five prayers. And we refute it by accepting the consequence of his cause, saying: According to us, it is valid only with specified intention. We allow it with absolute intention on the basis that it is specification. That is, we concede that specification is necessary for obligatory acts, but specification is of two types: specification from the side of the servant by intention, and specification from the side of the Lawgiver. This absolute (intention) is in the ruling of specification from the side of the Lawgiver, for He said: 'When half of Sha'ban passes, there is no fasting except for Ramadan.' If the opponent says: 'Intentional specification is what is considered according to us, as in compensatory and expiatory fasts, not absolute specification.'
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage introduces a key topic in Hanafi usul al-fiqh: the classification of legal causes ('ilal) into two types: tardiyyah (concomitant) and mu'aththirah (effective). The Shafi'is rely on tardiyyah causes, which are descriptive attributes that accompany the ruling without necessarily being the effective cause. The Hanafis refute such causes by a method called 'qawl bi-mujib al-illa' (accepting the consequence of the cause). This method involves conceding the opponent's premise but showing that it does not lead to the desired conclusion. The example given is the debate on the necessity of specifying the intention (niyyah) for fasting in Ramadan. The Shafi'is argue that because it is an obligatory fast, one must explicitly specify 'I intend to fast tomorrow for Ramadan,' analogizing to other obligatory fasts like qada' and kaffarah. The Hanafi response is to accept that specification is required, but to distinguish between two types of specification: intentional specification by the servant (which is not required) and legal specification by the Lawgiver (which is fulfilled by the mere fact that it is Ramadan). They cite the hadith 'When half of Sha'ban passes, there is no fasting except for Ramadan' to show that the Lawgiver has made the month itself a specification. Thus, the Hanafi position allows an absolute intention (e.g., 'I intend to fast tomorrow') without explicit mention of Ramadan, because the month itself provides the specification. This refutation forces the Shafi'is to either accept that their tardiyyah cause is ineffective or concede the Hanafi position. The passage also notes that the science of debate (ilm al-munazarah) was derived from this usul discussion.

Key Terms
علة طردية (illa tardiyyah): A legal cause that is merely concomitant (tard) with the ruling, not necessarily effective in producing it
علة مؤثرة (illa mu'aththirah): A legal cause that is effective in producing the ruling, having a suitable attribute (munasib)
قول بموجب العلة (qawl bi-mujib al-illa): A method of refutation where the objector accepts the necessary consequence of the opponent's cause while disputing the ruling

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example about fasting in Ramadan, what is the Shafi'i tardiyyah cause that the Hanafis refute via qawl bi-mujib?
A) The obligation (fardiyyah) of the fast
B) The month of Ramadan
C) The intention of the fasting person
D) The hadith about Sha'ban

Key Takeaway
Qawl bi-mujib al-illa is a powerful Hanafi refutation method that concedes the opponent's necessary consequence while maintaining the disagreement, forcing them to refine or abandon their tardiyyah cause.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
6 min
Illah Tardiyyah and Mu'aththirah, and al-Qawl bi-Mujib
Defining two types of 'illah and explaining the first method of defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tardiyyah and mu'aththirah 'illah and apply the method of al-qawl bi-mujib to defend against a tardiyyah cause in a Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute.

Core Idea
A cause that merely describes but does not effect can be repelled by accepting its consequence while preserving the dispute.

This lesson defines two types of 'illah used in qiyas: tardiyyah (superficial, merely descriptive) and mu'aththirah (effective, ratio legis). Hanafis require mu'aththirah, while Shafi'is accept tardiyyah. The first method to defend against a tardiyyah 'illah is al-qawl bi-mujib, which tactically concedes the necessary consequence while maintaining disagreement on the ruling. We will work through the Ramadan fast example to see this in action.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of 'illah
Distinguish between tardiyyah (descriptive, superficial) and mu'aththirah (effective). Note which school uses each.
Tardiyyah is insufficient for Hanafis; it must be mu'aththirah.
2
Understand al-qawl bi-mujib
This defense tactic accepts the necessary consequence of the opponent's 'illah but still contests the specific ruling. It forces the opponent to show that the 'illah produces the ruling directly.
It is not a concession of defeat.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i argument
The Shafi'i uses tardiyyah 'illah: the fast of Ramadan is obligatory; wherever obligatoriness is found (e.g., make-up fasts), specification of intention is required; thus Ramadan requires explicit mention of 'Ramadan' in the intention.
4
Apply the Hanafi response
Using al-qawl bi-mujib, the Hanafi concedes that specification is necessary for an obligatory fast but distinguishes intentional specification from specification by the Lawgiver. The Lawgiver has specified Ramadan through the hadith, so absolute intention suffices.
Cite the hadith: 'When Sha'ban ends, there is no fast except for Ramadan.'
5
Recognize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis accept absolute intention (e.g., 'I intend to fast tomorrow') for Ramadan; Shafi'is require explicit mention of 'Ramadan'.

Source Text

ثُمَّ العِلل نَوْعَانِ طَرْدِيَةٌ وَمُؤَثِّرَةٌ. فَإِنَّ الطَّرْدِيَةَ لِلشَّافَعِيَةِ وَنَحْنُ نَدْفَعُهَا عَلَى وَجْهٍ يُلْحِقُهُمْ إِلَى الْقَوْلِ بِالتَّأْثِيرِ. وَالْمُؤَثِّرَةُ لَنَا وَتَدْفَعُهَا الشَّافَعِيَّةُ ثُمَّ نُجِيبُهُمْ عَنِ الدَّفْعِ. وَهَذَا الْبَحْثُ هُوَ أَسَاسُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ وَالْمُحَاوَرَةِ. أَمَّا الطَّرْدِيَةُ فَوُجُوهٌ دَفْعُهَا أَرْبَعَةٌ: الْقَوْلُ بِمُوجِبِ الْعِلَّةِ، أَيِ الْتِزَامُ مَا يَلْزَمُ الْمُعَلِّلُ بِتَعْلِيلِهِ مَعَ بَقَاءِ الْخِلَافِ فِي الْحُكْمِ الْمُتَنَازَعِ فِيهِ. كَقَوْلِهِمْ فِي صَوْمِ رَمَضَانَ إِنَّهُ صَوْمُ فَرْضٍ فَلَا يُؤَدَّى إِلَّا بِتَعْيِينِ النِّيَّةِ. فَأَرَادُوا الْعِلَّةَ الطَّرْدِيَّةَ، وَهِيَ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ، لِلتَّعْيِينِ إِذْ كُلَّمَا وُجِدَتِ الْفَرْضِيَّةُ وُجِدَ التَّعْيِينُ كَصَوْمِ الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ وَالصَّلَوَاتِ الْخَمْسِ. وَنَحْنُ نَدْفَعُهُ بِمُوجِبِ عِلَّتِهِ فَنَقُولُ: عِنْدَنَا لَا يَصِحُّ إِلَّا بِتَعْيِينِ النِّيَّةِ، وَإِنَّمَا نُجَوِّزُهُ بِإِطْلَاقِ النِّيَّةِ عَلَى أَنَّهُ تَعْيِينٌ. أَيْ سَلَّمْنَا أَنَّ التَّعْيِينَ ضَرُورِيٌّ لِلْفَرَضِ، وَلَكِنَّ التَّعْيِينَ نَوْعَانِ: تَعْيِينٌ مِنْ جَانِبِ الْعِبَادِ قَصْدًا، وَتَعْيِينٌ مِنْ جَانِبِ الشَّارِعِ. هَذَا الْإِطْلَاقُ فِي حُكْمِ التَّعْيِينِ مِنْ جَانِبِ الشَّارِعِ فَإِنَّهُ قَالَ: إِذَا انْسَلَخَ شَعْبَانُ فَلَا صَوْمَ إِلَّا عَنْ رَمَضَانَ. فَإِنْ قَالَ الْخَصْمُ: إِنَّ التَّعْيِينَ الْقَصْدِيَّ هُوَ الْمُعْتَبَرُ عِنْدَنَا كَمَا فِي الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ دُونَ التَّعْيِينِ مُطْلَقًا. فَنَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ التَّعْيِينَ الْقَصْدِيَّ مُعْتَبَرٌ، وَلَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ عِلَّةَ التَّعْيِينِ الْقَصْدِيِّ فِي الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ هِيَ مُجَرَّدُ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ، بَلْ كَوْنُ وَقْتِهِ صَالِحًا لِأَنْوَاعٍ.
Then the causes are of two types: tardiyyah (superficial) and mu'aththirah (effective). Indeed, the tardiyyah is for the Shafi'is, and we repel it in a way that forces them to accept the effective. And the effective is for us, and the Shafi'is repel it, then we answer them regarding the repulsion. And this discussion is the foundation of debate and dialogue. As for the tardiyyah, its repulsion has four ways: al-qawl bi-mujib al-'illah, i.e., accepting what the causaist (mu'allil) necessitates by his causation while maintaining disagreement in the disputed ruling. Like their statement concerning the fast of Ramadan that it is an obligatory fast, so it is not performed except with specifying the intention. They intended the tardiyyah cause, which is obligatoriness, for specification, since whenever obligatoriness is found, specification is found, like the fast of make-up, expiation, and the five prayers. And we repel it by the necessary consequence of his cause, so we say: According to us, it is not valid except with specifying the intention, but we permit it with absolute intention on the basis that it is specification. That is, we concede that specification is necessary for the obligatory, but specification is of two types: specification from the side of the servants intentionally, and specification from the side of the Lawgiver. This absolutness is in the ruling of specification from the side of the Lawgiver, for He said: 'When Sha'ban ends, there is no fast except for Ramadan.' If the opponent says: 'Intentional specification is what is considered according to us, as in make-up and expiation, not specification absolutely.' We say: We do not concede that intentional specification is considered, and we do not concede that the cause of intentional specification in make-up and expiation is mere obligatoriness, but rather that its time is suitable for various types.
Source pages p. 181

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and methodological exposition from a Hanafi usul text. It identifies two types of 'illah (legal cause) in qiyas: tardiyyah (descriptive, superficial) and mu'aththirah (effective, ratio legis). The author states that the Shafi'is use tardiyyah 'illah, which merely accompanies the ruling without being its effective cause, while the Hanafis use mu'aththirah 'illah, which is the actual effective cause. The Hanafis defend against the Shafi'i use of tardiyyah by forcing them to admit that their cause must be effective. Then the text focuses on the first method of repelling a tardiyyah 'illah: al-qawl bi-mujib al-'illah (accepting the consequence of the cause). This means the Hanafi debater concedes that the consequence of the Shafi'i's 'illah applies, but still maintains the disagreement on the specific ruling. The example given: Shafi'is argue that the fast of Ramadan requires specifying the intention (e.g., saying 'I intend to fast tomorrow for Ramadan') because it is a fast with obligatoriness (fardiyyah), and they claim that wherever fardiyyah exists, specification is required, as in make-up fasts, expiation fasts, and the five prayers. The Hanafi repels this by accepting that specification is necessary, but distinguishes two types: specification by the worshipper's explicit intent, and specification by the Lawgiver's designation. In Ramadan, the Lawgiver has specified it by the arrival of the month, so an absolute intention (e.g., 'I intend to fast tomorrow') is considered specification because the time is exclusively for Ramadan. If the Shafi'i objects that intentional specification is what is required (as in make-up and expiation), the Hanafi replies that the cause in those cases is not mere obligatoriness but the fact that the time is suitable for various types of fasts (unlike Ramadan which is exclusive). Thus, the Hanafi refutes the tardiyyah 'illah by accepting its consequence but showing that the specific ruling does not follow, preserving the Hanafi position that an absolute intention suffices for Ramadan.

Key Terms
العِلَّة الطَّرْدِيَّة (al-'illah al-tardiyyah): A cause that is merely descriptive, consistent with the ruling but not proven to be effective (mu'aththira); used by Shafi'is.
العِلَّة المُؤَثِّرَة (al-'illah al-mu'aththirah): A cause that is effective in producing the ruling, proven by proper evidence; used by Hanafis.
القَوْلُ بِمُوجِبِ العِلَّة (al-qawl bi-mujib al-'illah): A method of refutation where the respondent accepts the necessary consequence of the opponent's 'illah but still maintains the disagreement on the specific ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi defense against a tardiyyah 'illah using al-qawl bi-mujib, what does the Hanafi debater accept?
A) The entire Shafi'i ruling and evidence.
B) The necessary consequence of the Shafi'i's 'illah while still disagreeing on the specific ruling.
C) That the 'illah is mu'aththirah.
D) That the intention must be explicit and specific.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul distinguishes between tardiyyah and mu'aththirah 'illah, and al-qawl bi-mujib is a key tactic to defend against tardiyyah by conceding the consequence but preserving the dispute.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Refuting a Defective Cause: Al-Qawl bi-Mujab al-‘Illah
Explaining the method of al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa with the Ramadan fast example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa to refute a defective causal argument by conceding the opponent's causal premise but adding a distinction that preserves your own ruling.

Core Idea
Qawl bi-mujab: accept the opponent's causal premise, then redirect by distinguishing the basis of the ruling.

In Hanafi dialectical method (munāẓara), a common tactic to refute a weak cause (al-‘illa al-ṭardiyya) is to concede its surface-level implication while incorporating a distinction that preserves one's own position. This method, called al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa, is illustrated by the Hanafi response to the Shafi‘i argument that Ramadan fasting requires a specific intention. The Hanafis agree that specification is needed but argue that the specification can come from the Lawgiver, not just from the servant. This allows the general intention to suffice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's causal argument
Determine the opponent's claimed cause and how it connects to the ruling. Here, the Shafi'i argues: Ramadan fast is obligatory, and all obligatory fasts require specific intention, so Ramadan must have specific intention.
The cause is the obligation (fardiyya) as a common factor.
2
Concede the apparent causal relationship
Accept that the opponent's premise (specification needed) applies in principle. 'We agree that fasting requires specification.'
This is the 'qawl bi-mujab' step – you accept the implication of the opponent's cause.
3
Introduce a distinction to redirect the ruling
Show that the opponent's cause is not sufficient because there is a further distinction: specification can come from the Lawgiver (Ramadan is already specified) or from the servant. Since Ramadan is already specified by the Shari‘ah, a general intention suffices.
The distinction must be valid and based on evidence, not arbitrary.
4
Apply the distinction to preserve your ruling
Conclude that the opponent's argument does not require specific intention in this case because the specification is already provided by the Lawgiver.
This method is considered weak because you do not reject the opponent's premise; you only add a nuance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أمَّا الطَّرْدِيَّةُ الْمَخْدُومِ؟ فَدَفْعُهَا بِأَرْبَعَةِ طُرُقٍ: (1) الْقَوْلُ بِمُوجَبِ الْعِلَّةِ: وَهُوَ التَّسْلِيمُ بِظَاهِرِ مَا ثَبَتَ بِالْعِلَّةِ الَّتِي أَتَى بِهَا الْخَصْمُ، مَعَ إِثْبَاتِ الْحُكْمِ الْمُتَنَازَعِ فِيهِ خِلَافَ مَا أَرَادَ الْخَصْمُ، كَقَوْلِ الشَّافِعِيِّ فِي صَوْمِ رَمَضَانَ: إِنَّهُ فَرْضٌ، فَلَا يَصِحُّ بِدُونِ تَعْيِينٍ فِي النِّيَّةِ، كَصَوْمِ الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ. فَنَقُولُ: عِنْدَنَا لَا يَصِحُّ إِلَّا بِالتَّعْيِينِ، لَكِنَّ التَّعْيِينَ نَوْعَانِ: تَعْيِينٌ بِقَصْدِ الْعَبْدِ، وَتَعْيِينٌ مِنْ جِهَةِ الشَّارِعِ. فَرَمَضَانُ مُتَعَيِّنٌ بِالشَّرْعِ، فَإِطْلَاقُ النِّيَّةِ كَافٍ. فَإِنِ اعْتَرَضَ الْخَصْمُ بِأَنَّ التَّعْيِينَ الْقَصْدِيَّ هُوَ الْمُعْتَبَرُ، فَنَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ عِلَّةَ التَّعْيِينِ الْقَصْدِيِّ هِيَ مُجَرَّدُ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ، بَلْ لِأَنَّ وَقْتَ الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ صَالِحٌ لِأَنْوَاعِ الصِّيَامِ، بِخِلَافِ رَمَضَانَ فَإِنَّهُ مُتَعَيِّنٌ كَالْمُتَوَحِّدِ فِي الْمَكَانِ يُصَابُ بِمُطْلَقِ اسْمِهِ. وَلَمْ يَذْكُرْ هَذَا الِاعْتِرَاضَ أَهْلُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ لِأَنَّهُ سَطْحِيٌّ لَا يَبْقَى بَعْدَ الدَّقَّةِ وَتَعْيِينِ الْبَحْثِ، فَإِنَّ اسْتِفْسَارَ الْمُدَّعِي عِنْدَهُمْ وَبَيَانَهُ بَعْدَ الطَّلَبِ وَاجِبٌ فَلَا يُقْبَلُ قَطُّ. (2) الْمُمَانَعَةُ: وَهِيَ عَدَمُ قَبُولِ السَّائِلِ مُقَدَّمَاتِ دَلِيلِ الْمُعَلِّلِ كُلِّهَا أَوْ بَعْضِهَا بِالتَّعْيِينِ وَالتَّفْصِيلِ، وَهِيَ أَرْبَعَةٌ بِالِاسْتِقْرَاءِ لِأَنَّهَا إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ فِي نَفْسِ الْوَصْفِ، أَيْ لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ هَذَا الْوَصْفَ الَّذِي تَدَّعِيهِ وَصْفًا عِلَّةٌ بَلِ الْعِلَّةُ شَيْءٌ آخَرُ، كَقَوْلِ الشَّافِعِيِّ فِي كَفَّارَةِ الْإِفْطَارِ: إِنَّهَا عُقُوبَةٌ مُتَعَلِّقَةٌ بِالْجِمَاعِ فَلَا تَجِبُ فِي الْأَكْلِ وَالشُّرْبِ. فَنَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ فِي الْأَصْلِ الْجِمَاعُ بَلِ الْإِفْطَارُ عَمْدًا، وَهُوَ حَاصِلٌ فِي الْأَكْلِ وَالشُّرْبِ أَيْضًا، بِدَلِيلِ أَنَّهُ لَوْ جَامَعَ نَاسِيًا لَا يُفْسِدُ صَوْمَهُ لِعَدَمِ الْإِفْطَارِ أَصْلًا. وَالْقِسْمُ الثَّانِي: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ هَذَا الْوَصْفَ صَالِحٌ لِلْحُكْمِ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ مَوْجُودًا، كَقَوْلِ الشَّافِعِيِّ فِي إِثْبَاتِ الْوِلَايَةِ عَلَى الْبِكْرِ: إِنَّهَا بَاكِرَةٌ جَاهِلَةٌ بِأَمْرِ النِّكَاحِ لِعَدَمِ الْمُمَارَسَةِ بِالرِّجَالِ فَيُوَلَّى عَلَيْهَا. فَنَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ وَصْفَ الْبَكَارَةِ صَالِحٌ لِهَذَا الْحُكْمِ لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَظْهَرْ لَهُ تَأْثِيرٌ فِي مَوْضِعٍ آخَرَ، بَلِ الصَّالِحُ لَهُ هُوَ الصِّغَرُ.
As for the defective [tardiyya] cause, repelling it is by four methods: (1) Al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa: accepting the apparent consequence of the opponent's causal argument while establishing the disputed ruling contrary to what the opponent intended, like the Shafi'i's statement about Ramadan fast: it is obligatory, so it is not valid without specification in intention, like the make-up fast and expiation fast. So we say: According to us, it is not valid except with specification, but specification is of two types: specification by the servant's intention, and specification from the Lawgiver's side. Ramadan is specified by the Law, so absolute intention suffices. If the opponent objects that intentional specification is what is considered, we say: We do not accept that the cause of intentional specification is mere obligation, but rather because the time of make-up and expiation is suitable for various types of fasts, unlike Ramadan, which is specified like a solitary person in a place, hit by his absolute name. The scholars of disputation did not mention this objection because it is superficial and does not remain after precision and specification of the topic, for the clarification of the claimant according to them after request is obligatory, so it is never accepted. (2) Al-mumana'a: it is the refusal of the questioner to accept all or some of the premises of the proof of the causal arguer with specification and detail. It is of four types by induction, because it is either regarding the description itself: we do not accept that this description you claim is a cause, but the cause is something else, like the Shafi'i's statement about expiation for breaking the fast: it is a punishment connected to intercourse, so it is not obligatory for eating and drinking. So we say: We do not accept that the cause in the original is intercourse, but rather breaking the fast intentionally, and that is present in eating and drinking as well, evidenced by the fact that if he forgetfully has intercourse, his fast is not invalidated due to no breaking of the fast at all. The second type: we do not accept that this description is suitable for the ruling while it exists, like the Shafi'i's statement about establishing guardianship over a virgin: she is a virgin ignorant of marriage matters due to lack of interaction with men, so guardianship is established over her. So we say: We do not accept that the attribute of virginity is suitable for this ruling because it has shown no effect in another context, rather what is suitable is minority.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary on the topic of munazara (dialectical disputation). It explains the methods of refuting a 'illa tardiyya — a causal argument that is defective or insufficient. The author details two of the four methods. The first method is al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa (accepting the consequence of the opponent's causal premise). Here, the Hanafi respondent concedes the opponent's causal link but adds a distinction that preserves his own position. The example given is the Shafi'i argument that since Ramadan fast is obligatory, it requires a specific intention, just like make-up and expiation fasts. The Hanafi response is to accept that specification is required but to distinguish between specification by the servant and specification by the Lawgiver. Because Ramadan is already specified by the Shari‘ah, a general intention suffices. The author notes that this method is considered superficial by scholars of munazara and is not relied upon. The second method is al-mumana'a (denial), which is a more substantive refutation. It involves denying one or more premises of the opponent's argument. The author lists two subtypes under mumana'a: denial that the alleged description is the actual cause (the example of expiation for breaking fast: the Shafi'i says the cause is intercourse, but the Hanafi says it is intentional breaking of the fast, which includes eating and drinking), and denial that the description is suitable for the ruling (the example of guardianship over a virgin: the Shafi'i says virginity is the cause, but the Hanafi says it is minority that is the suitable cause, as virginity shows no effect elsewhere). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of istidlal (argumentation) and the sophisticated approach to responding to opponent's causal arguments.

Key Terms
العِلَّةُ الطَّرْدِيَّةُ (al-‘illat al-ṭardiyya): A defective cause that does not meet the conditions of a valid legal cause (such as being effective in other similar cases).
الْقَوْلُ بِمُوجَبِ الْعِلَّةِ (al-qawl bi-mūjab al-‘illa): A refutation method where the respondent accepts the apparent consequence of the opponent's causal premise but then establishes the disputed ruling differently.
الْمُمَانَعَةُ (al-mumāna‘a): A refutation method where the respondent denies one or more premises of the opponent's proof, either the description being the cause or its suitability for the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument that Ramadan fast requires specific intention?
A) They deny that specification is needed at all.
B) They accept that specification is needed but argue that Ramadan is already specified by the Lawgiver.
C) They argue that intention is not required for obligatory acts.
D) They claim that the Shafi'i argument is invalid because it is based on a weak hadith.

Key Takeaway
Al-qawl bi-mujab is a method of refutation that concedes the opponent's causal link but adds a distinction to preserve one's own ruling, though it is considered superficial in advanced munazara.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Refuting a Defective Cause: Al-Mumāna‘a (Denial of Premises)
Explaining the method of al-mumāna‘a with two examples from fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the method of al-mumāna‘a to deny an opponent's causal premises by either rejecting the description as the cause or denying its suitability for the ruling.

Core Idea
Mumāna‘a: deny the opponent's causal premise either by rejecting the alleged cause or its suitability for the ruling.

A more robust refutation of a defective cause is al-mumāna‘a, where the respondent outright denies one or more premises of the opponent's proof. This passage presents two types: denial that the claimed description is in fact the cause, and denial that the description is suitable (effective) for the ruling. These are illustrated with examples from expiation for breaking fast (Hanafi vs Shafi‘i) and guardianship over a virgin. The denial must be specific and evidence-based to be effective.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's causal argument and its premises
Every causal argument has at least two key premises: (a) that a certain description exists and is the cause, and (b) that this description is suitable for the ruling (i.e., it has legal effect).
In the expiation example, the Shafi'i premises: (a) intercourse is the cause for expiation; (b) it is suitable because it is a punishable act.
2
Choose which premise to deny
Either deny that the description is the cause (offering an alternative cause) or deny that the description is suitable (showing it lacks effect elsewhere).
Denial must be specific and detailed ('with specification and detail').
3
Apply denial of the description as cause
In the expiation example, the Hanafi denies that intercourse is the cause; rather, the cause is intentional breaking of the fast. Evidence: forgetful intercourse does not invalidate the fast, showing that intent is essential.
The alternative cause must be supported by evidence.
4
Apply denial of the description's suitability
In the guardianship example, the Hanafi denies that virginity is suitable for guardianship because it has no effect in other contexts. Instead, minority is the suitable cause.
Suitability often requires the cause to show efficacy in similar cases.
5
Conclude the refutation
If the denial is accepted, the opponent's argument fails. If the opponent rebuts, further argumentation ensues.
A successful mumāna‘a completely dismantles the opponent's proof.

Source Text

أمَّا الطَّرْدِيَّةُ الْمَخْدُومِ؟ فَدَفْعُهَا بِأَرْبَعَةِ طُرُقٍ: (1) الْقَوْلُ بِمُوجَبِ الْعِلَّةِ: وَهُوَ التَّسْلِيمُ بِظَاهِرِ مَا ثَبَتَ بِالْعِلَّةِ الَّتِي أَتَى بِهَا الْخَصْمُ، مَعَ إِثْبَاتِ الْحُكْمِ الْمُتَنَازَعِ فِيهِ خِلَافَ مَا أَرَادَ الْخَصْمُ، كَقَوْلِ الشَّافِعِيِّ فِي صَوْمِ رَمَضَانَ: إِنَّهُ فَرْضٌ، فَلَا يَصِحُّ بِدُونِ تَعْيِينٍ فِي النِّيَّةِ، كَصَوْمِ الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ. فَنَقُولُ: عِنْدَنَا لَا يَصِحُّ إِلَّا بِالتَّعْيِينِ، لَكِنَّ التَّعْيِينَ نَوْعَانِ: تَعْيِينٌ بِقَصْدِ الْعَبْدِ، وَتَعْيِينٌ مِنْ جِهَةِ الشَّارِعِ. فَرَمَضَانُ مُتَعَيِّنٌ بِالشَّرْعِ، فَإِطْلَاقُ النِّيَّةِ كَافٍ. فَإِنِ اعْتَرَضَ الْخَصْمُ بِأَنَّ التَّعْيِينَ الْقَصْدِيَّ هُوَ الْمُعْتَبَرُ، فَنَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ عِلَّةَ التَّعْيِينِ الْقَصْدِيِّ هِيَ مُجَرَّدُ الْفَرْضِيَّةِ، بَلْ لِأَنَّ وَقْتَ الْقَضَاءِ وَالْكَفَّارَةِ صَالِحٌ لِأَنْوَاعِ الصِّيَامِ، بِخِلَافِ رَمَضَانَ فَإِنَّهُ مُتَعَيِّنٌ كَالْمُتَوَحِّدِ فِي الْمَكَانِ يُصَابُ بِمُطْلَقِ اسْمِهِ. وَلَمْ يَذْكُرْ هَذَا الِاعْتِرَاضَ أَهْلُ الْمُنَاظَرَةِ لِأَنَّهُ سَطْحِيٌّ لَا يَبْقَى بَعْدَ الدَّقَّةِ وَتَعْيِينِ الْبَحْثِ، فَإِنَّ اسْتِفْسَارَ الْمُدَّعِي عِنْدَهُمْ وَبَيَانَهُ بَعْدَ الطَّلَبِ وَاجِبٌ فَلَا يُقْبَلُ قَطُّ. (2) الْمُمَانَعَةُ: وَهِيَ عَدَمُ قَبُولِ السَّائِلِ مُقَدَّمَاتِ دَلِيلِ الْمُعَلِّلِ كُلِّهَا أَوْ بَعْضِهَا بِالتَّعْيِينِ وَالتَّفْصِيلِ، وَهِيَ أَرْبَعَةٌ بِالِاسْتِقْرَاءِ لِأَنَّهَا إِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ فِي نَفْسِ الْوَصْفِ، أَيْ لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ هَذَا الْوَصْفَ الَّذِي تَدَّعِيهِ وَصْفًا عِلَّةٌ بَلِ الْعِلَّةُ شَيْءٌ آخَرُ، كَقَوْلِ الشَّافِعِيِّ فِي كَفَّارَةِ الْإِفْطَارِ: إِنَّهَا عُقُوبَةٌ مُتَعَلِّقَةٌ بِالْجِمَاعِ فَلَا تَجِبُ فِي الْأَكْلِ وَالشُّرْبِ. فَنَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ الْعِلَّةَ فِي الْأَصْلِ الْجِمَاعُ بَلِ الْإِفْطَارُ عَمْدًا، وَهُوَ حَاصِلٌ فِي الْأَكْلِ وَالشُّرْبِ أَيْضًا، بِدَلِيلِ أَنَّهُ لَوْ جَامَعَ نَاسِيًا لَا يُفْسِدُ صَوْمَهُ لِعَدَمِ الْإِفْطَارِ أَصْلًا. وَالْقِسْمُ الثَّانِي: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ هَذَا الْوَصْفَ صَالِحٌ لِلْحُكْمِ مَعَ كَوْنِهِ مَوْجُودًا، كَقَوْلِ الشَّافِعِيِّ فِي إِثْبَاتِ الْوِلَايَةِ عَلَى الْبِكْرِ: إِنَّهَا بَاكِرَةٌ جَاهِلَةٌ بِأَمْرِ النِّكَاحِ لِعَدَمِ الْمُمَارَسَةِ بِالرِّجَالِ فَيُوَلَّى عَلَيْهَا. فَنَقُولُ: لَا نُسَلِّمُ أَنَّ وَصْفَ الْبَكَارَةِ صَالِحٌ لِهَذَا الْحُكْمِ لِأَنَّهُ لَمْ يَظْهَرْ لَهُ تَأْثِيرٌ فِي مَوْضِعٍ آخَرَ، بَلِ الصَّالِحُ لَهُ هُوَ الصِّغَرُ.
As for the defective [tardiyya] cause, repelling it is by four methods: (1) Al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa: accepting the apparent consequence of the opponent's causal argument while establishing the disputed ruling contrary to what the opponent intended, like the Shafi'i's statement about Ramadan fast: it is obligatory, so it is not valid without specification in intention, like the make-up fast and expiation fast. So we say: According to us, it is not valid except with specification, but specification is of two types: specification by the servant's intention, and specification from the Lawgiver's side. Ramadan is specified by the Law, so absolute intention suffices. If the opponent objects that intentional specification is what is considered, we say: We do not accept that the cause of intentional specification is mere obligation, but rather because the time of make-up and expiation is suitable for various types of fasts, unlike Ramadan, which is specified like a solitary person in a place, hit by his absolute name. The scholars of disputation did not mention this objection because it is superficial and does not remain after precision and specification of the topic, for the clarification of the claimant according to them after request is obligatory, so it is never accepted. (2) Al-mumana'a: it is the refusal of the questioner to accept all or some of the premises of the proof of the causal arguer with specification and detail. It is of four types by induction, because it is either regarding the description itself: we do not accept that this description you claim is a cause, but the cause is something else, like the Shafi'i's statement about expiation for breaking the fast: it is a punishment connected to intercourse, so it is not obligatory for eating and drinking. So we say: We do not accept that the cause in the original is intercourse, but rather breaking the fast intentionally, and that is present in eating and drinking as well, evidenced by the fact that if he forgetfully has intercourse, his fast is not invalidated due to no breaking of the fast at all. The second type: we do not accept that this description is suitable for the ruling while it exists, like the Shafi'i's statement about establishing guardianship over a virgin: she is a virgin ignorant of marriage matters due to lack of interaction with men, so guardianship is established over her. So we say: We do not accept that the attribute of virginity is suitable for this ruling because it has shown no effect in another context, rather what is suitable is minority.

Commentary
This passage is from a Hanafi usul al-fiqh commentary on the topic of munazara (dialectical disputation). It explains the methods of refuting a 'illa tardiyya — a causal argument that is defective or insufficient. The author details two of the four methods. The first method is al-qawl bi-mujab al-‘illa (accepting the consequence of the opponent's causal premise). Here, the Hanafi respondent concedes the opponent's causal link but adds a distinction that preserves his own position. The example given is the Shafi'i argument that since Ramadan fast is obligatory, it requires a specific intention, just like make-up and expiation fasts. The Hanafi response is to accept that specification is required but to distinguish between specification by the servant and specification by the Lawgiver. Because Ramadan is already specified by the Shari‘ah, a general intention suffices. The author notes that this method is considered superficial by scholars of munazara and is not relied upon. The second method is al-mumana'a (denial), which is a more substantive refutation. It involves denying one or more premises of the opponent's argument. The author lists two subtypes under mumana'a: denial that the alleged description is the actual cause (the example of expiation for breaking fast: the Shafi'i says the cause is intercourse, but the Hanafi says it is intentional breaking of the fast, which includes eating and drinking), and denial that the description is suitable for the ruling (the example of guardianship over a virgin: the Shafi'i says virginity is the cause, but the Hanafi says it is minority that is the suitable cause, as virginity shows no effect elsewhere). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi method of istidlal (argumentation) and the sophisticated approach to responding to opponent's causal arguments.

Key Terms
العِلَّةُ الطَّرْدِيَّةُ (al-‘illat al-ṭardiyya): A defective cause that does not meet the conditions of a valid legal cause (such as being effective in other similar cases).
الْقَوْلُ بِمُوجَبِ الْعِلَّةِ (al-qawl bi-mūjab al-‘illa): A refutation method where the respondent accepts the apparent consequence of the opponent's causal premise but then establishes the disputed ruling differently.
الْمُمَانَعَةُ (al-mumāna‘a): A refutation method where the respondent denies one or more premises of the opponent's proof, either the description being the cause or its suitability for the ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two types of al-mumāna‘a mentioned in the passage?
A) Denial of the existence of the description and denial of the ruling.
B) Denial that the description is the cause and denial that the description is suitable for the ruling.
C) Denial of the opponent's authority and denial of the evidence.
D) Denial of the entire argument and denial of a part of the argument.

Key Takeaway
Al-mumāna‘a is a strong refutation that denies the opponent's causal premises, requiring specific and detailed arguments.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
The Four Types of Mumana'a and Fasād al-Waḍ‘ in Qiyas
Explaining the four forms of objection (mumana'a) against an opponent's claimed 'illa

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify each type of mumana'a, apply them to a given qiyas argument, and distinguish fasād al-waḍ‘ from the other four.

Core Idea
Mumana'a is the structured legal objection: deny the 'illa's existence, suitability, ruling, attribution, or intrinsic repugnance.

In usul al-fiqh, not every claim of an effective cause ('illa) stands unchallenged. The Hanafi school has developed a precise taxonomy of objections (mumana'a) that a scholar can raise against an opponent's qiyas. This lesson, drawn from the commentary on Nur al-Anwar, walks you through the four classical types and the additional category of fasād al-waḍ‘, each illustrated with real examples from Hanafi-Shafi'i debates.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of mumana'a
Recognize that mumana'a is a legitimate rebuttal in qiyas when the opponent's claimed 'illa is defective. It is not arbitrary denial but based on specific grounds.
The term literally means 'obstruction' or 'prevention'.
2
Study the first two types: denial of existence and denial of suitability
Examine the examples provided: the kaffara case (denial of existence of intercourse as 'illa) and the virginity case (denial of suitability of virginity as 'illa). Note how the Hanafi responds with alternative explanations.
In the kaffara case, the Hanafi uses forgetful intercourse as proof that intentional breaking is the true 'illa.
3
Study the third and fourth types: denial of the ruling and denial of attribution
Analyze the wiping of the head example: the Shafi'i claims thrice is sunnah because it is an integral; the Hanafi denies the ruling (sunnah is completion, not thrice) and denies attribution (thrice is not attached to being an integral).
The fourth type often overlaps with the second but focuses on the link between ruling and attribute.
4
Explore the fifth type: fasād al-waḍ‘
Understand that this objection challenges the attribute as intrinsically repugnant to the ruling. The example: Islam causing separation contradicts its protective nature.
This type is not mentioned by ahl al-munazara but is subsumed under 'failure of taqrīb'.
5
Practice identifying each type
Given a statement of an opponent's 'illa, classify which type of mumana'a could be raised and justify your choice.
Focus on the precise point of objection: attribute existence, suitability, ruling itself, attribution, or intrinsic repugnance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ممانعت کی چار صورتیں نکلتی ہیں۔
الف۔ نفس وصف کو تسلیم کرنے سے انکار کر دینا یعنی یہ کہ ہم اس وصف کو تسلیم ہی نہیں کرتے جس کا مدعی وصف علت ہونے کا دعویٰ کرتا ہے۔ بلکہ علت دوسری ہے۔ جیسے امام شافعی کا فرمان، روزہ افطار کرنے سے کفارہ کے بارے میں، کہ یہ (کفارہ) سزا ہے جو جماع سے متعلق ہے لہذا کل و شرب میں یہ کفارہ واجب نہ ہو گا۔ تو ہم جواب دیتے ہیں کہ ہم اس کو تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ اصل میں علت جماع ہے، بلکہ عمداً افطار کر لینا اس کی علت ہے اور وہ اکل و شرب میں بھی پائی جاتی ہے اور اس کی دلیل یہ ہے کہ یہی جماع اگر بھول کر سر زد ہو جائے تو صوم فاسد نہیں ہوتا۔ اس لئے کہ جماع ہے مگر افطار نہیں پایا گیا۔
(۲) یا وصف کا وجود تسلیم کر کے اس کے صالح للحکم ہونے کا انکار کرنا یعنی یہ کہنا کہ ہم اس کو تسلیم نہیں کرتے کہ یہ وصف حکم کی علت بننے کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے باوجودیکہ وصف موجود بھی ہے۔ جیسے کہ امام شافعی کا قول باکرہ میں ولایت کا ثابت کرنا کہ یہ عورت باکرہ ہے امور نکاح سے ناواقف ہے کیونکہ مردوں کے ساتھ اس کا رابطہ نہیں رہا لہذا اولی اس کا والی بالنکاح ہو گا۔ تو ہم اس کے جواب میں کہتے ہیں کہ اس مسئلے میں وصف بکارہ ایسی علت اور وصف ہے جو اس حکم کی صلاحیت رکھتا ہے۔ کیونکہ دوسرے مقام میں وصف بکارہ کی تاثیر ظاہر نہیں ہوئی۔ بلکہ باکرہ میں ولایت کی علت لڑکی کا صغیرہ ہونا ہے۔ یون علت صغر پائے جانے کی وجہ سے حکم ولایت ثابت ہوا ہے۔ نہ کہ بکارہ کے وصف کی وجہ سے۔ اور صغر کا اثر ولایت بالمال میں ظاہر ہو چکا ہے کیونکہ مال صغیرہ پر ولی کو ولایت حاصل ہوتی ہے۔
(۳) أو في نفس الحكم: أى لا نسلم أن هذا الحكم حكم بل الحكم شيء آخر. كقول الشافعي في مسح الرأس أنه ركن في الوضوء فيسن تثليثه كغسل الوجه. فنقول: لا نسلم أن المسنون في الوضوء التثليث بل الإكمال بعد تمام الفرض. ففي الوجه لما استوعب الفرض صار إلى التثليث. وفي الرأس لما لم يستوعب الفرض الرأس صار إلى الإكمال فيكون هو السنة دون التثليث.
(٤) أو في نسبته إلى الوصف: أى لا نسلم أن هذا الحكم منسوب إلى هذا الوصف بل إلى وصف آخر. مثل أن نقول في المسألة المذكورة: لا نسلم أن التثليث في الغسل مضاف إلى الركنية بدليل الانتقاض بالقيام والقراءة فإنهما ركنان في الصلاة ولا يسن تثليثها. ولمصعة والاستنشاق حيث يسن تثليثها بلا ركنية.
 وَفَساد الوضع - وهو كون الوصف في نفسه بحيث يكون آبيًا عن الحكم ومقتضاه لصده. ولم يذكره أهل المناظرة. ويمكن درجه فيما قالوا. إنه لا يتم التقريب. كتعليمهم أى تعليل الشافعية لإيجاب الفرقة بإسلام أحد الزوجين. فإنهم قالوا إذا أسلم أحد الزوجين الكافرين تقع الفرقة بينهما بمجرد الإسلام إن كانت غير مدخول بها وبعد مضي ثلاث حيض إن كانت مدخولاً بها ولا يحتاج إلى أن يعرض الإسلام على الآخر. ونحن نقول هذا في وضعه فاسد لأن الإسلام عرف عاصمًا للحقوق لا رافعًا لها. فينبغي أن يعرض الإسلام على الآخر فإن أسلم بقي النكاح بينهما وإلا تضاف الفرقة إلى إباء الآخر. وهو معنى معقول صحيح.
There are four forms of objection (mumana'a). 
First: Denying the existence of the attribute itself — that we do not acknowledge the attribute that the claimant claims is the cause, but rather the cause is something else. Like the statement of Imam al-Shafi'i regarding kaffara for breaking fast: that it (kaffara) is a penalty related to sexual intercourse, so it would not be obligatory in eating and drinking. So we reply: we do not acknowledge that the cause is intercourse; rather, intentionally breaking fast is the cause, and that also occurs in eating and drinking. And its proof is that if intercourse occurs forgetfully, the fast is not nullified because intercourse occurred but breaking fast did not occur.
Second: Or acknowledging the existence of the attribute but denying its suitability for the ruling — i.e., saying we do not acknowledge that this attribute has the capacity to be the cause of the ruling, despite the attribute being present. Like the statement of Imam al-Shafi'i regarding establishing guardianship in a virgin: that this woman is a virgin and unaware of marriage matters because she has not had contact with men, therefore her guardian will be the guardian in marriage. So we reply in response: in this issue, the attribute of virginity is not an attribute that has the capacity for this ruling, because in another instance the effect of virginity has not appeared. Rather, the cause of guardianship in a virgin is that the girl is a minor. Thus, the ruling of guardianship is established due to the presence of minority, not due to the attribute of virginity. And the effect of minority has already appeared in guardianship over property because the guardian has authority over the minor's wealth.
Third: Or denying the ruling itself — i.e., we do not acknowledge that this ruling is a ruling; rather the ruling is something else. Like the statement of al-Shafi'i regarding wiping the head: that it is an integral part of ablution, so doing it thrice is sunnah like washing the face. So we say: we do not acknowledge that the sunnah in ablution is thrice, rather it is completion after finishing the obligatory part. In the face, since the obligatory part encompasses it, he went to thrice; in the head, since the obligatory part does not encompass the head, he goes to completion, so that is the sunnah, not thrice.
Fourth: Or denying its attribution to the attribute — i.e., we do not acknowledge that this ruling is attributed to this attribute, but to another attribute. For example, we say in the aforementioned issue: we do not acknowledge that thrice in washing is attributed to being an integral part, evidenced by the objection with standing and recitation, for they are integrals in prayer yet they are not sunnah to do thrice. And with madmada and istinshaq, where thrice is sunnah without being integral.
And fasād al-waḍ‘ — that is, the attribute being in itself such that it repels the ruling and its implication because it contradicts it. The people of disputation (ahl al-munāẓara) did not mention it, and it is possible to include it in what they said: 'Indeed it does not complete the taqrīb.' Like their teaching — i.e., the justification of the Shafi'is for imposing separation upon the conversion of one of the spouses: for they said that if one of the two infidel spouses converts to Islam, separation occurs between them merely by Islam if she has not been consummated, and after three menstrual cycles if she has been consummated, and there is no need to present Islam to the other. And we say: this in its foundation is invalid, because Islam is known to protect rights, not to remove them. So it is appropriate to present Islam to the other; if he converts, the marriage remains between them, otherwise separation is attributed to the other's refusal. And that is a sound rational meaning.
Source pages p. 184

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Nur al-Anwar explains the four types of objection (mumana'a) that a Hanafi scholar can raise against an opponent's claimed cause ('illa) in a qiyas argument. The author classifies these as: (1) rejecting the very existence of the attribute claimed as 'illa, (2) admitting the attribute but denying its suitability for the ruling, (3) rejecting the ruling itself, and (4) rejecting that the ruling is attached to that particular attribute. Each type is illustrated with examples from the Shafi'i school, showing how the Hanafi counter-argument works. The first example concerns kaffara for breaking fast: Shafi'i argues that the cause is intercourse, so eating/drinking wouldn't incur kaffara; Hanafi rebuttal says the cause is intentional breaking of fast, which includes eating. The second example concerns guardianship: Shafi'i says virginity is the cause; Hanafi says it is minority. The third example involves wiping the head: Shafi'i says thrice is sunnah because it is an integral; Hanafi says completion is sunnah. The fourth example again uses the same issue to argue that thrice is not attributed to being an integral. After these four, the author introduces a third way to rebut a claimed 'illa called 'fasād al-waḍ‘' — that the attribute in itself is incompatible with the ruling. This was not mentioned by earlier scholars of disputation, but can be subsumed under 'lack of completion of the taqrīb' (i.e., the analogy is flawed). The example given is the Shafi'i ruling that if one spouse converts, separation occurs immediately. Hanafi argues this is invalid because Islam protects rights; so Islam should be presented to the other spouse. This passage is primarily a rule for handling objections in qiyas, and it demonstrates Hanafi usul methodology.

Key Terms
ممانعة (mumāna'a): Objection to the opponent's claimed 'illa by denying its existence, suitability, ruling, or attribution.
علة ('illa): The effective cause that links the original case to the new case in qiyas.
فساد الوضع (fasād al-waḍ‘): The attribute being intrinsically repugnant to the ruling, making it impossible to be the 'illa.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Shafi'i argument for kaffara (kaffara) on breaking fast, what attribute did they claim as the 'illa, and how did the Hanafi respond?
A) Shafi'i claimed eating; Hanafi said it is intercourse.
B) Shafi'i claimed intercourse; Hanafi said it is intentional breaking of the fast.
C) Shafi'i claimed forgetfulness; Hanafi said it is deliberate.
D) Shafi'i claimed nighttime; Hanafi said it is daytime.

Key Takeaway
Mumana'a is a rigorous tool in qiyas; knowing its categories equips you to systematically challenge flawed causal claims.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Munāqaḍa: Contradiction as a Dialectical Refutation
Defines munāqaḍa and illustrates with a dialectical exchange

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define munāqaḍa and apply it to critique a qiyās argument by identifying a counterexample (naqḍ).

Core Idea
Munāqaḍa forces an opponent to either abandon their cause or provide a valid distinguishing factor.

This passage introduces munāqaḍa (contradiction) as a key dialectical tool in Hanafi usul. It shows how a counterexample (naqḍ) can undermine a claimed cause, shifting the burden of proof. The example involves a Shafi'i argument that intention must be obligatory in wudū' because it is obligatory in tayammum, both being purifications. The Hanafi response uses munāqaḍa by pointing to washing a garment—another purification—where intention is not required.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claimed cause (ʿilla)
Find the attribute that the opponent claims is the cause of the ruling. In the passage, the Shafi'i claims that 'being a purification' is the cause for intention being obligatory.
2
Find a counterexample (naqḍ)
Search for a case that shares the claimed attribute but has a different ruling. The Hanafi give the example of washing a garment: it is also a purification for prayer, yet intention is not required.
This naqḍ shows that 'being a purification' does not consistently lead to obligation of intention.
3
Apply the dialectical move (munāqaḍa)
Point out the contradiction: the claimed cause fails to produce the same ruling in a parallel case. This forces the opponent to either abandon the cause or provide a distinguishing factor (farq).
Munāqaḍa is synonymous with manʿ (prevention), as it prevents the opponent's qiyās from being accepted.
4
Analyze the opponent's attempted distinction
The opponent may try to differentiate the cases. In the passage, the Shafi'i argues that wudū' removes legal impurity (unintelligible) while washing a garment removes physical impurity (intelligible). The Hanafi rebut that the removal of impurity from the body after urine is also intelligible.
The Hanafi response shows that the distinction fails, strengthening the munāqaḍa.
5
Recognize the incomplete argument
The passage ends with a discussion of hardship (ḥaraj) to explain why ghusl is required only for semen and not urine. This suggests a deeper Hanafi counter-distinction based on frequency of occurrence.
The incomplete text reminds us to be cautious with OCR‑damaged sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمُنَاقِضَةُ وهِنْ تَخْتَلِفُ الْحُكْمُ عَنْ الْوَصفِ الَّذِي ادْغِى كَوْتَهُ عِلَّةً ويُعَبْرُ عَنْ هَذا في علم المناظرة بالنقض وأمَّا المُناقِضَةُ فَى مُرَادِفَةٌ عِنْدَهُمْ لِلْمَنْعِ كَقَوْلِ الشافعِي فِي الْوَصُوْمِ وَاللَّيْمُ إِنَّهُما طَهَارَتَانِ فَكَيْفَ افْتَرِقَا فِي النِّيَةِ لا يَفْتَرِقَانِ فِي النِّيَةٍ فَإِذَا كَانَتِ النِّيَةُ فَرْضًا فِي التيمم بِالإِثْمَانِ فَتَكُونَ فِي الوَضَوْءِ كَذَالِكَ فَإِنَّهُ يَنْتَقِضُ بِغْسَلِ النُّوْبِ وَالْبَدَنِ فَإِنَّهُ أَيْضًا طَهَارَةً لِلصلوة فِى أَنْ تُقْرَضَ النِّيَةُ فِيهِ فَلا بُدَّ حِينَئِذٍ أَن يَلْجى الخصم إِلَى بَيَانِ الْفَرْقِ بَيْنَهُما الْخَصَمُ وَالْقَوْلُ بالتَّاثِرِ بِأَنْ غَسَّلَ الثَّوْبِ طَهَارَةٌ حَقِيقِيَة وَإِزَالَةُ الْجِنْسِ حَقِيقِي وَهُوَ مَعْقُول لايحتاج إلى النّيَّةِ بِخِلافِ الوَضَيْ فَإِنَّهُ طَهَارَةٌ لِنَجْسِ حُكْمِي وَهُوَ غَيْرُ مَعْقُول فَيَحْتَاجُ إلى النّيَةِ كَالتيمم فَنَقُولُ فِي جَوَابِهِ إِنْ زَوَالَ الطَّهَارَةِ بَعْدَ خُرُوجِ النَّجِسِ أَمْرٌ مَعْقُول لأَنَّ الْبَدَنَ كُلُّهُ يَتَنَجْسُ يُخْرِجُ الْبَوْلِ وَالْمَعْنى بِستَوَاء وَلَكِن لَمَّا كَانَ المنى أقل إخْرَاجًا وَجَبَ الْغُسلِ فِيْهِ لِتَمَامِ البِدَنِ بِالأَخْرَجَ بِخِلَافِ الْبَوْلِ فَإِنَّهُ لَمَا كَان أَكْثَرُ خُرُوجًا وفى غسل كل ال بِكُلِّ مَرَّةٍ حَرَجٌ عَظَيْمٌ لَأَجَرَمَ يُقْتَصِرُ عَلَى الأعْضاء الأربعة التي هي أَصَوْلُ الْبَدَنِ فِي الْحُدُودِ حدود وقوع الأنام منه دفعًا للحـ للحرج
أَي
And munāqaḍa: that the ruling differs from the attribute which is claimed to be the cause, and this is expressed in the science of dialectics as 'naqḍ' (counterexample). As for munāqaḍa, it is synonymous with 'manʿ' (prevention) among them, like the statement of al-Shafi'i regarding tayammum and wudu': they are both purifications, so how do they differ in intention? They do not differ in intention. For if intention is obligatory in tayammum based on consensus, then it should be so in wudu' likewise. But this is contradicted by washing a garment and the body, for that is also purification for prayer, yet intention is not obligatory in it. So then the opponent must resort to showing the difference between them and saying 'the effect' [ta'thīr], that washing a garment is a real purification and removal of real filth, which is intelligible and does not require intention, unlike wudu', which is purification from legal impurity and is not intelligible, therefore it requires intention like tayammum. So we say in response: The removal of impurity after the exit of filth is an intelligible matter, because the whole body becomes impure whether urine or semen exits, and the meaning is the same. However, since semen is lesser in exit, ghusl is obliged for it for the entire body due to the exit, unlike urine, because it is greater in exit and washing the whole body every time would be great hardship. Therefore it is confined to the four limbs which are the principal parts of the body in the limits... to avoid hardship.
Source pages p. 186

Commentary
This passage defines munāqaḍa (contradiction) as a method of refutation in dialectics: it occurs when the ruling differs from the attribute claimed to be the cause. The author equates munāqaḍa with manʿ (prevention). The example given is the Shafi'i argument that tayammum and wudu' are both purifications, so if intention is obligatory in tayammum, it should also be obligatory in wudu'. The Hanafi response uses munāqaḍa by pointing out that washing a garment or the body (ghusl) is also a purification for prayer but intention is not obligatory there. This forces the opponent to show a distinction. The Hanafi then provide their own distinction: washing a garment is real purification from real filth, so it is intelligible and does not need intention. Wudu' purifies from legal impurity, which is not intelligible, so it requires intention like tayammum. However, the author notes that this distinction is challenged: the removal of impurity after filth exit is intelligible for both urine and semen, but the difference is that ghusl is required only for semen (which is less frequent) to avoid hardship, while for urine (more frequent), only washing the four limbs is required to avoid hardship. The passage ends abruptly, indicating incomplete reasoning.

Key Terms
المناقضة (al-munāqaḍa): Contradiction: a method of refutation where the claimed cause is shown to conflict with another ruling that shares the same attribute.
النقض (al-naqḍ): Counterexample: a specific instance that contradicts the claimed causal relationship.
المنع (al-manʿ): Prevention: the same as munāqaḍa, used to prevent acceptance of an opponent's causal claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the synonym of munāqaḍa mentioned in the passage?
A) Naqḍ
B) Farq
C) Manʿ
D) Qiyās

Key Takeaway
Munāqaḍa is a powerful dialectical tool that exposes contradictions in causal claims by presenting counterexamples, shifting the burden of proof to the opponent.

The Munaqada Objection: Intention in Wudu vs Tayammum
Analyzing munaqada objection and Hanafi response on intention

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a munaqada objection and apply the Hanafi defense of 'farq' (distinction) to preserve a qiyas.

Core Idea
Munaqada challenges qiyas by showing cause present but ruling absent; Hanafi defense distinguishes the two cases by their rational nature.

In usul al-fiqh, a mu'allil (one who establishes an analogy) must defend their qiyas against objections. One common objection is munaqada, where the opponent points to a case where the claimed cause (illa) is present, yet the ruling is different, suggesting the cause is not effective. In this passage, the Hanafi school defends its position that intention is not required in wudu, by distinguishing wudu from tayammum based on the intrinsic property of water versus soil. This lesson dissects that debate to sharpen your skills in handling munaqada objections.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the Hanafi position on intention in wudu
What is the Hanafi ruling regarding niyyah (intention) for the validity of wudu? Is it wajib, sunnah, or fard? State it clearly.
The Hanafis hold intention is not fard or wajib but recommended (sunnah).
2
2. Identify the Shafi'i qiyas and the munaqada objection
The Shafi'i school analogizes wudu to tayammum: both are purifications (tahara), so they should share the ruling regarding intention. Since intention is required in tayammum by consensus, it must be required in wudu. This is the munaqada: the claimed cause (tahara) is present in both, yet Hanafis rule differently.
Munaqada shows inconsistency in the mu'allil's own system.
3
3. Analyze the Hanafi response: what distinction (farq) do they make?
The Hanafis argue that the nature of tayammum (soil) is inherently impure (maluth) and not purifying by nature, whereas water is naturally purifying. Hence, intention is needed to transform soil into a purifying agent, but water's action is rational (ma'qul) and does not require intention.
This farq breaks the analogy—the cause (tahara) is not identical in both cases.
4
4. Explain why the farq blocks the munaqada
A successful munaqada requires that the illa is genuinely present in both cases. The Hanafi farq shows that the illa (tahara) is achieved differently in wudu (by water's nature) and tayammum (by intention), so the munaqada fails.
The mu'allil can defend by showing the 'effect' (ta'thir) of the cause is case-specific.
5
5. Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
In Hanafi fiqh, wudu without intention is valid; in Shafi'i fiqh, it is invalid. This difference affects daily practice.
The dispute is not merely theoretical; it has actual implications for worship.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَالأَقْتِصَارُ عَلَى الأَعْضَاءِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ غَيْرُ مَعْقُول. وَالْمُنَافِضَةُ كَقَوْلِ الشَّافِعِي فِي الْوُضُوءِ وَالتَّيَمُّمِ: فَإِنَّهُ يَنْتَقِضُ بِغَسْلِ الثَّوْبِ وَالْبَدَنِ. فَنَقُولُ فِي الْجَوَابِ: وَأَمَّا نَجَاسَةُ الْبَدَنِ وَإِزَالَةُ الْمَاءِ فَأَمْرٌ مَعْقُولٌ فَلَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى النِّيَّةِ بِخِلَافِ التُّرَابِ لِأَنَّهُ مَلُوثٌ فِي نَفْسِهِ غَيْرُ مُطَهِّرٍ بِطَبْعِهِ فَلِذَا يَحْتَاجُ إِلَى النِّيَّةِ.
Restricting to the four limbs is unreasonable. And the contradiction (munaqada) like the statement of al-Shafi'i concerning wudu and tayammum: it is invalidated by washing the garment and the body. So we say in response: As for impurity of the body and removal by water, it is a reasonable matter, so it does not need intention, unlike soil because it is itself contaminated, not purifying by its nature, therefore it needs intention.
Source pages p. 187

Commentary
This passage is a dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is over the necessity of intention (niyyah) in wudu. The Shafi'i argues that since both wudu and tayammum are purifications, they should be treated alike; thus if intention is required in tayammum (by consensus), it must also be required in wudu. This is an objection of munaqada (contradiction) because the Shafi'i claims the Hanafi rule on wudu lacks intention despite the shared cause (ta'hara). The Hanafi response distinguishes: washing the body from actual impurity is rational (ma'qul) because water naturally cleans, so no intention needed. But tayammum uses soil which is inherently impure (maluth) and not purifying by nature, thus requiring intention. This distinction (farq) based on the nature of the purifying agent defends against the munaqada objection.

Key Terms
المناقضة (al-munāqaḍah): An objection where the opponent shows that the claimed cause (illa) is present but the ruling is absent in another case
فساد الوضع (fasād al-waḍ‘): An objection where the opponent shows that the very establishment of the cause is invalid, e.g., the qiyas itself is flawed
قول بالتأثير (qawl bi-al-ta’thīr): The claim that the cause has an effective impact on the ruling, usually supported by evidence from text, consensus, or reason

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school argue that intention is not required in wudu, according to the passage?
A) Because water is naturally purifying and the act is rational, unlike tayammum on soil.
B) Because the Quran explicitly states it.
C) Because wudu is not an act of worship.
D) Because the Shafi'i school has no valid proof.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi defense against munaqada is to show a meaningful difference (farq) between the two cases that explains why the ruling differs.
